   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Plasma (physics)
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>

        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

         <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">

         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">
          neon lights
         </a>
         are commonplace generators of plasma. Bottom left: A
         <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">
          plasma globe
         </a>
         , illustrating some of the more complex plasma phenomena, including
         <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)#Filamentation" title="Plasma (physics)">
          filamentation
         </a>
         . Bottom right: A plasma trail from the
         <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">
          Space Shuttle
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">
          <i>
           Atlantis
          </i>
         </a>
         during re-entry into
         <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
          Earth's atmosphere
         </a>
         , as seen from the
         <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">
          International Space Station
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Plasma
       </b>
       (from
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        πλάσμα
       </span>
       , meaning "moldable substance"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-πλάσμα_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-πλάσμα-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) is one of
       <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter#The_four_fundamental_states" title="State of matter">
        the four fundamental states of matter
       </a>
       , and was first described by chemist
       <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">
        Irving Langmuir
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in the 1920s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012a_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012a-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . Unlike the other three states,
       <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
        solid
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">
        liquid
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">
        gas
       </a>
       , plasma does not exist freely on the Earth's surface under normal conditions. Plasma can only be artificially generated by heating or subjecting a neutral gas to a strong
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">
        electromagnetic field
       </a>
       to the point an ionised gaseous substance becomes increasingly
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">
        electrically conductive
       </a>
       , and long-range electromagnetic fields dominate the behavior of the matter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012b_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012b-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Plasma and ionised gases have unique properties and display behaviors unlike those of the other states, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">
        transition
       </a>
       between them is mostly a matter of nomenclature
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and subject to interpretation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on the surrounding environmental
       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
        temperature
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       , partially
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">
        ionised
       </a>
       or fully ionised forms of plasma may be produced.
       <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">
        Neon signs
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
        lightning storms
       </a>
       are examples of partially ionised plasma,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the interior of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       is an example of fully ionised plasma,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       along with the
       <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">
        solar corona
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschwanden2004_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aschwanden2004-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piel2010_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Piel2010-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Positive
       <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">
        charges
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">
        ions
       </a>
       is achieved by stripping away
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">
        electrons
       </a>
       orbiting the atomic nuclei, where the total number of electrons removed is only related to either increasing temperature or the local density of other ionised matter. This also can be accompanied by the dissociation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_bond" title="Molecular bond">
        molecular bonds
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturrock_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sturrock-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , though this process is distinctly different from
       <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">
        chemical processes
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Metal_ions_in_aqueous_solution" title="Metal ions in aqueous solution">
        ion interactions in liquids
       </a>
       or the behavior of shared ions in
       <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">
        metals
       </a>
       . The response of plasma to electromagnetic fields can be usefully employed in many modern technological devices, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_display" title="Plasma display">
        plasma televisions
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_etching" title="Plasma etching">
        plasma etching
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itptma2013_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itptma2013-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Plasma may be the most abundant form of
       <a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">
        ordinary matter
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">
        universe
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itptma2013a_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itptma2013a-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although this hypothesis is currently tentative based on the existence and unknown properties of
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">
        dark matter
       </a>
       . Plasma is mostly associated with
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       , extending to the rarefied
       <a href="/wiki/Intracluster_medium" title="Intracluster medium">
        intracluster medium
       </a>
       and possibly the
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">
        intergalactic regions
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoPA2015_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BoPA2015-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The word
       <i>
        plasma
       </i>
       comes from the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        πλάσμα
       </span>
       , meaning "moldable substance"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-πλάσμα_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-πλάσμα-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or "jelly",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whose usage describes the behaviour of the ionised atomic nuclei and the electrons within the surrounding region of the plasma. Very simply, each of these nuclei are suspended in a movable sea of electrons. Plasma was first identified in a
       <a href="/wiki/Crookes_tube" title="Crookes tube">
        Crookes tube
       </a>
       , and so described by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_William_Crookes" title="Sir William Crookes">
        Sir William Crookes
       </a>
       in 1879 (he called it "radiant matter").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nature of this "
       <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray" title="Cathode ray">
        cathode ray
       </a>
       " matter was subsequently identified by British physicist
       <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">
        Sir J.J. Thomson
       </a>
       in 1897.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The term "plasma" was coined by
       <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">
        Irving Langmuir
       </a>
       in 1928.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langmuir1928_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-langmuir1928-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Lewi_Tonks" title="Lewi Tonks">
        Lewi Tonks
       </a>
       and Harold Mott-Smith, both of whom worked with Irving Langmuir in the 1920s, recall that Langmuir first used the word "plasma" in analogy with blood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mott-Smith recalls, in particular, that the transport of electrons from thermionic filaments reminded Langmuir of “the way blood plasma carries red and white corpuscles and germs.”
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Langmuir described the plasma he observed as follows:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "
        <i>
         Except near the electrodes, where there are
        </i>
        sheaths
        <i>
         containing very few electrons, the ionised gas contains ions and electrons in about equal numbers so that the resultant space charge is very small. We shall use the name
        </i>
        plasma
        <i>
         to describe this region containing balanced charges of ions and electrons.
        </i>
        "
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langmuir1928_16-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-langmuir1928-16">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">
       <tr>
        <th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em">
         <a href="/wiki/Continuum_mechanics" title="Continuum mechanics">
          Continuum mechanics
         </a>
        </th>
       </tr>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_parameters">
        Properties and parameters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Properties and parameters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_fountain.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="400" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif/200px-Plasma_fountain.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif/300px-Plasma_fountain.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_fountain.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's
         <a href="/wiki/Artistic_rendering" title="Artistic rendering">
          rendition
         </a>
         of the Earth's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_fountain" title="Plasma fountain">
          plasma fountain
         </a>
         , showing oxygen, helium, and hydrogen ions that gush into space from regions near the Earth's poles. The faint yellow area shown above the north pole represents gas lost from Earth into space; the green area is the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_borealis" title="Aurora borealis">
          aurora borealis
         </a>
         , where plasma energy pours back into the atmosphere.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
          <a href="#cite_note-20">
           [20]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">
        Definition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Definition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Plasma is a
       <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">
        state of matter
       </a>
       in which an ionised gaseous substance becomes highly
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">
        electrically conductive
       </a>
       to the point that long-range
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">
        electric and magnetic fields
       </a>
       dominate the behavior of the matter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2–3_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2–3-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This state can be contrasted with the
       <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter#The_four_fundamental_states" title="State of matter">
        other states
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
        solid
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">
        liquid
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">
        gas
       </a>
       . Unlike these other states of matter, plasma mostly does not naturally exist on the Earth's surface under normal conditions, and must be artificially generated from neutral gases.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012b_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012b-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Plasma is an electrically neutral medium of unbound positive and negative particles (i.e. the overall charge of a plasma is roughly zero). Although these particles are unbound, they are not ‘free’ in the sense of not experiencing forces. Moving charged particles generate an electric current within a magnetic field, and any movement of a charged plasma particle affects and is affected by the fields created by the other charges. In turn this governs collective behavior with many degrees of variation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturrock_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sturrock-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Three factors define a plasma:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hazeltine_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hazeltine-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <b>
         The plasma approximation
        </b>
        : The plasma approximation applies when the plasma parameter, Λ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
         <a href="#cite_note-26">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
        representing the number of charge carriers within a sphere (called the Debye sphere whose radius is the Debye screening length) surrounding a given charged particle, is sufficiently high as to shield the electrostatic influence of the particle outside of the sphere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2–3_21-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2–3-21">
          [21]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121_22-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Bulk interactions
        </b>
        : The Debye screening length (defined above) is short compared to the physical size of the plasma. This criterion means that interactions in the bulk of the plasma are more important than those at its edges, where boundary effects may take place. When this criterion is satisfied, the plasma is quasineutral.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
         <a href="#cite_note-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Plasma frequency
        </b>
        : The electron plasma frequency (measuring
        <a href="/wiki/Plasma_oscillation" title="Plasma oscillation">
         plasma oscillations
        </a>
        of the electrons) is large compared to the electron-neutral collision frequency (measuring frequency of collisions between electrons and neutral particles). When this condition is valid, electrostatic interactions dominate over the processes of ordinary gas kinetics.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
         <a href="#cite_note-28">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ol>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Degree_of_ionisation">
        Degree of ionisation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Degree of ionisation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       For plasma to exist,
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">
        ionisation
       </a>
       is necessary. The term "plasma density" by itself usually refers to the "electron density", that is, the number of free electrons per unit volume. The
       <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_ionization" title="Degree of ionization">
        degree of ionisation
       </a>
       of a plasma is the proportion of atoms that have lost or gained electrons, and is controlled mostly by the temperature. Even a partially ionised gas in which as little as 1% of the particles are ionised can have the characteristics of a plasma (i.e., response to magnetic fields and high
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">
        electrical conductivity
       </a>
       ). The degree of ionisation,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

       </span>
       is the number density of electrons.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Temperatures">
        Temperatures
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Temperatures">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Nonthermal_plasma" title="Nonthermal plasma">
        Nonthermal plasma
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Anisothermal_plasma" title="Anisothermal plasma">
        Anisothermal plasma
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Plasma temperature is commonly measured in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvins" title="Kelvins">
        kelvins
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">
        electronvolts
       </a>
       and is, informally, a measure of the thermal kinetic energy per particle. High temperatures are usually needed to sustain ionisation, which is a defining feature of a plasma. The degree of plasma ionisation is determined by the
       <a href="/wiki/Electron_temperature" title="Electron temperature">
        electron temperature
       </a>
       relative to the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">
        ionization energy
       </a>
       (and more weakly by the density), in a relationship called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saha_equation" title="Saha equation">
        Saha equation
       </a>
       . At low temperatures, ions and electrons tend to recombine into bound states—atoms
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nicholson-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —and the plasma will eventually become a gas.
      </p>
      <p>
       In most cases the electrons are close enough to
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">
        thermal equilibrium
       </a>
       that their temperature is relatively well-defined, even when there is a significant deviation from a
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
        Maxwellian
       </a>
       energy
       <a href="/wiki/Distribution_function" title="Distribution function">
        distribution function
       </a>
       , for example, due to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">
        UV radiation
       </a>
       , energetic particles, or strong
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_fields" title="Electric fields">
        electric fields
       </a>
       . Because of the large difference in mass, the electrons come to thermodynamic equilibrium amongst themselves much faster than they come into equilibrium with the ions or neutral atoms. For this reason, the ion temperature may be very different from (usually lower than) the electron temperature. This is especially common in weakly ionised technological plasmas, where the ions are often near the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambient_temperature" title="Ambient temperature">
        ambient temperature
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_vs._nonthermal_plasmas">
        Thermal vs. nonthermal plasmas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Thermal vs. nonthermal plasmas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Based on the relative temperatures of the electrons, ions and neutrals, plasmas are classified as "thermal" or "non-thermal". Thermal plasmas have electrons and the heavy particles at the same temperature, i.e. they are in thermal equilibrium with each other. Nonthermal plasmas on the other hand have the ions and neutrals at a much lower temperature (sometimes room temperature), whereas electrons are much "hotter" (
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle T_{e}\gg T_{n}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                e
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              ≫
              <!-- ≫ -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                n
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle T_{e}\gg T_{n}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="T_e \gg T_n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2843eb098a1abed6f66b9af0a0ca1023b90c8ebb" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.546ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Complete_vs._incomplete_ionisation">
        Complete vs. incomplete ionisation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Complete vs. incomplete ionisation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A plasma is sometimes referred to as being "hot" if it is nearly fully ionised, or "cold" if only a small fraction (for example 1%) of the gas molecules are ionised, but other definitions of the terms "hot plasma" and "cold plasma" are common. Even in a "cold" plasma, the electron temperature is still typically several thousand degrees Celsius. Plasmas utilized in "plasma technology" ("technological plasmas") are usually cold plasmas in the sense that only a small fraction of the gas molecules are ionised.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_potential">
        Plasma potential
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plasma potential">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/220px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/330px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/440px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
          Lightning
         </a>
         is an example of plasma present at Earth's surface. Typically, lightning discharges 30,000 amperes at up to 100 million volts, and emits light, radio waves, X-rays and even gamma rays.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
          <a href="#cite_note-32">
           [32]
          </a>
         </sup>
         Plasma temperatures in lightning can approach 28,000 K (28,000 °C; 50,000 °F) and electron densities may exceed 10
         <sup>
          24
         </sup>
         m
         <sup>
          −3
         </sup>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Since plasmas are very good
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">
        electrical conductors
       </a>
       , electric potentials play an important role.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2017)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The potential as it exists on average in the space between charged particles, independent of the question of how it can be measured, is called the "plasma potential", or the "space potential". If an electrode is inserted into a plasma, its potential will generally lie considerably below the plasma potential due to what is termed a
       <a href="/wiki/Debye_sheath" title="Debye sheath">
        Debye sheath
       </a>
       . The good electrical conductivity of plasmas makes their electric fields very small. This results in the important concept of "quasineutrality", which says the density of negative charges is approximately equal to the density of positive charges over large volumes of the plasma (
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle n_{e}=\langle Z\rangle n_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                e
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
              ⟨
              <!-- ⟨ -->
             </mo>
             <mi>
              Z
             </mi>
             <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
              ⟩
              <!-- ⟩ -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                i
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle n_{e}=\langle Z\rangle n_{i}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="n_e = \langle Z\rangle n_i" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1193ff1fbe003293adab62d868adbe4d2555174" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.176ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       ), but on the scale of the
       <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">
        Debye length
       </a>
       there can be charge imbalance. In the special case that
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_layer_(plasma)" title="Double layer (plasma)">
         double layers
        </a>
       </i>
       are formed, the charge separation can extend some tens of Debye lengths.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The magnitude of the potentials and electric fields must be determined by means other than simply finding the net
       <a href="/wiki/Charge_density" title="Charge density">
        charge density
       </a>
       . A common example is to assume that the electrons satisfy the
       <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_relation" title="Boltzmann relation">
        Boltzmann relation
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle n_{e}\propto e^{e\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                n
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ∝
               <!-- ∝ -->
              </mo>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                e
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 Φ
                 <!-- Φ -->
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mo>
                  /
                 </mo>
                </mrow>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  k
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   B
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  T
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   e
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle n_{e}\propto e^{e\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="n_{e}\propto e^{e\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7469b31fe8ad01e161bccadd28bccea4e189fb6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.008ex; height:3.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Differentiating this relation provides a means to calculate the electric field from the density:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {E}}=(k_{B}T_{e}/e)(\nabla n_{e}/n_{e}).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mover>
                 <mi>
                  E
                 </mi>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  →
                  <!-- → -->
                 </mo>
                </mover>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                k
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                /
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               e
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               ∇
               <!-- ∇ -->
              </mi>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                n
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                /
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                n
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\vec {E}}=(k_{B}T_{e}/e)(\nabla n_{e}/n_{e}).}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\vec{E} = (k_BT_e/e)(\nabla n_e/n_e)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/631406a82915c1f6add73c3592d1f7cbf2dc459c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.317ex; height:3.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       It is possible to produce a plasma that is not quasineutral. An electron beam, for example, has only negative charges. The density of a non-neutral plasma must generally be very low, or it must be very small, otherwise it will be dissipated by the repulsive
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">
        electrostatic force
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrophysical" title="Astrophysical">
        astrophysical
       </a>
       plasmas,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_field_screening" title="Electric field screening">
        Debye screening
       </a>
       prevents
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric fields
       </a>
       from directly affecting the plasma over large distances, i.e., greater than the
       <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">
        Debye length
       </a>
       . However, the existence of charged particles causes the plasma to generate, and be affected by,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic fields
       </a>
       . This can and does cause extremely complex behavior, such as the generation of plasma double layers, an object that separates charge over a few tens of
       <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">
        Debye lengths
       </a>
       . The dynamics of plasmas interacting with external and self-generated
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic fields
       </a>
       are studied in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">
        academic discipline
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">
        magnetohydrodynamics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetization">
        Magnetization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Magnetization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Plasma with a magnetic field strong enough to influence the motion of the charged particles is said to be magnetized. A common quantitative criterion is that a particle on average completes at least one gyration around the magnetic field before making a collision, i.e.,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \omega _{\mathrm {ce} }/v_{\mathrm {coll} }&gt;1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               ω
               <!-- ω -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 c
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo>
               /
              </mo>
             </mrow>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               v
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 c
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 o
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 l
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 l
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              &gt;
             </mo>
             <mn>
              1
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \omega _{\mathrm {ce} }/v_{\mathrm {coll} }&gt;1}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\omega_{\mathrm{ce}} / v_{\mathrm{coll}} &gt; 1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/21f1862bc2cdc02a507ae2e79a1d0f3cdeabcca6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.388ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       , where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \omega _{\mathrm {ce} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               ω
               <!-- ω -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 c
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \omega _{\mathrm {ce} }}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\omega_{\mathrm{ce}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a77c94d2b62f2fbdb8c001c497f3987cf5b46d5c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.138ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the "electron gyrofrequency" and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{\mathrm {coll} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               v
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 c
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 o
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 l
                </mi>
                <mi mathvariant="normal">
                 l
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle v_{\mathrm {coll} }}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="v_{\mathrm{coll}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce453e7cf357667cbd5dec2fe68db30b003d6154" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.827ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the "electron collision rate". It is often the case that the electrons are magnetized while the ions are not. Magnetized plasmas are
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">
         anisotropic
        </a>
       </i>
       , meaning that their properties in the direction parallel to the magnetic field are different from those perpendicular to it. While electric fields in plasmas are usually small due to the high conductivity, the electric field associated with a plasma moving in a magnetic field is given by
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =-v\times \mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               E
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mi>
              v
             </mi>
             <mo>
              ×
              <!-- × -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               B
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathbf {E} =-v\times \mathbf {B} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\mathbf{E} = -v\times\mathbf{B}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/985221cedcc22c662aa027dda81134b51b67c10e" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.533ex; height:2.343ex;"/>
       </span>
       (where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {E} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               E
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathbf {E} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\mathbf {E} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d7f22b39d51f780fc02859059c1757c606b9de2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.757ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the electric field,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {v} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               v
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathbf {v} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\mathbf {v} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/35c1866e359fbfd2e0f606c725ba5cc37a5195d6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.411ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the velocity, and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold">
               B
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathbf {B} }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cafb0ef39b0f5ffa23c170aa7f7b4e718327c4d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.901ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the magnetic field), and is not affected by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debye_shielding" title="Debye shielding">
        Debye shielding
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_plasma_and_gas_phases">
        Comparison of plasma and gas phases
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Comparison of plasma and gas phases">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Plasma is often called the
       <i>
        fourth state of matter
       </i>
       after solid, liquids and gases, despite plasma typically being an ionised gas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is distinct from these and other lower-energy
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_matter" title="States of matter">
        states of matter
       </a>
       . Although it is closely related to the gas phase in that it also has no definite form or volume, it differs in a number of ways, including the following:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Property
        </th>
        <th>
         Gas
        </th>
        <th>
         Plasma
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">
          Electrical conductivity
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          Very low
         </b>
         : Air is an excellent insulator until it breaks down into plasma at electric field strengths above 30 kilovolts per centimeter.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
          <a href="#cite_note-39">
           [39]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Usually very high
         </b>
         : For many purposes, the conductivity of a plasma may be treated as infinite.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Independently acting species
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          One
         </b>
         : All gas particles behave in a similar way, influenced by
         <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
          gravity
         </a>
         and by
         <a href="/wiki/Collision" title="Collision">
          collisions
         </a>
         with one another.
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Two or three
         </b>
         :
         <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
          Electrons
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
          ions
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
          protons
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
          neutrons
         </a>
         can be distinguished by the sign and value of their
         <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
          charge
         </a>
         so that they behave independently in many circumstances, with different bulk velocities and temperatures, allowing phenomena such as new types of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waves_in_plasma" title="Waves in plasma">
          waves
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Instability" title="Instability">
          instabilities
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Velocity distribution
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
           Maxwellian
          </a>
         </b>
         : Collisions usually lead to a Maxwellian velocity distribution of all gas particles, with very few relatively fast particles.
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Often non-Maxwellian
         </b>
         : Collisional interactions are often weak in hot plasmas and external forcing can drive the plasma far from local equilibrium and lead to a significant population of unusually fast particles.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Interactions
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          Binary
         </b>
         : Two-particle collisions are the rule, three-body collisions extremely rare.
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Collective
         </b>
         : Waves, or organized motion of plasma, are very important because the particles can interact at long ranges through the electric and magnetic forces.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plasmas_in_astronomy_and_astrophysics">
        Plasmas in astronomy and astrophysics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Plasmas in astronomy and astrophysics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_plasma" title="Astrophysical plasma">
        Astrophysical plasma
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
        Interstellar medium
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergalactic_space" title="Intergalactic space">
        Intergalactic space
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Plasmas are by far the most common
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">
        phase of ordinary matter
       </a>
       in the universe, both by mass and by volume.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Within our Solar System,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_space" title="Interplanetary space">
        interplanetary space
       </a>
       is filled with the plasma expelled via the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       , extending from the Sun's surface out to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)#Heliopause" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">
        heliopause
       </a>
       . Furthermore, all the distant
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       , and much of
       <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
        interstellar space
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">
        intergalactic space
       </a>
       is also likely filled with plasma, albeit at very low densities.
       <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_plasma" title="Astrophysical plasma">
        Astrophysical plasmas
       </a>
       are also observed in
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">
        Accretion disks
       </a>
       around stars or compact objects like
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarfs
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       , or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_holes" title="Black holes">
        black holes
       </a>
       in close
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">
        binary star
       </a>
       systems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Plasma is associated with ejection of material in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jets" title="Astrophysical jets">
        astrophysical jets
       </a>
       , which have been observed with accreting black holes
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or in active
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">
        galaxies
       </a>
       like
       <a href="/wiki/Messier_87#Jet" title="Messier 87">
        M87's jet
       </a>
       that possibly extends out to 5,000 light-years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Common_plasmas">
        Common plasmas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Plasmas can appear in nature in various forms and locations, which can be usefully broadly summarised in the following Table:

           <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
