      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_plasma_phenomena">
        Complex plasma phenomena
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Complex plasma phenomena">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although the underlying equations governing plasmas are relatively simple, plasma behavior is extraordinarily varied and subtle: the emergence of unexpected behavior from a simple model is a typical feature of a
       <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">
        complex system
       </a>
       . Such systems lie in some sense on the boundary between ordered and disordered behavior and cannot typically be described either by simple, smooth, mathematical functions, or by pure randomness. The spontaneous formation of interesting spatial features on a wide range of length scales is one manifestation of plasma complexity. The features are interesting, for example, because they are very sharp, spatially intermittent (the distance between features is much larger than the features themselves), or have a
       <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">
        fractal
       </a>
       form. Many of these features were first studied in the laboratory, and have subsequently been recognized throughout the universe. Examples of complexity and complex structures in plasmas include:
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Filamentation">
        Filamentation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Filamentation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Striations or string-like structures,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">
        Birkeland currents
       </a>
       , are seen in many plasmas, like the
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">
        plasma ball
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
        aurora
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
        lightning
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">
        electric arcs
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flares" title="Solar flares">
        solar flares
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">
        supernova remnants
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are sometimes associated with larger current densities, and the interaction with the magnetic field can form a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_rope" title="Magnetic rope">
        magnetic rope
       </a>
       structure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       High power microwave breakdown at atmospheric pressure also leads to the formation of filamentary structures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwbrkdwn_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mwbrkdwn-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (See also
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_pinch" title="Plasma pinch">
        Plasma pinch
       </a>
       )
      </p>
      <p>
       Filamentation also refers to the self-focusing of a high power laser pulse. At high powers, the nonlinear part of the index of refraction becomes important and causes a higher index of refraction in the center of the laser beam, where the laser is brighter than at the edges, causing a feedback that focuses the laser even more. The tighter focused laser has a higher peak brightness (irradiance) that forms a plasma. The plasma has an index of refraction lower than one, and causes a defocusing of the laser beam. The interplay of the focusing index of refraction, and the defocusing plasma makes the formation of a long filament of plasma that can be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometer_(unit)" title="Micrometer (unit)">
        micrometers
       </a>
       to kilometers in length.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One interesting aspect of the filamentation generated plasma is the relatively low ion density due to defocusing effects of the ionised electrons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (See also
       <a href="/wiki/Filament_propagation" title="Filament propagation">
        Filament propagation
       </a>
       )
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-neutral_plasma">
        Non-neutral plasma
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Non-neutral plasma">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The strength and range of the electric force and the good conductivity of plasmas usually ensure that the densities of positive and negative charges in any sizeable region are equal ("quasineutrality"). A plasma with a significant excess of charge density, or, in the extreme case, is composed of a single species, is called a
       <a href="/wiki/Non-neutral_plasmas" title="Non-neutral plasmas">
        non-neutral plasma
       </a>
       . In such a plasma, electric fields play a dominant role. Examples are charged
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_beam" title="Particle beam">
        particle beams
       </a>
       , an electron cloud in a
       <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">
        Penning trap
       </a>
       and positron plasmas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Dusty_plasma.2Fgrain_plasma">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dusty_plasma/grain_plasma">
        Dusty plasma/grain plasma
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dusty plasma/grain plasma">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Dusty_plasma" title="Dusty plasma">
        dusty plasma
       </a>
       contains tiny charged particles of dust (typically found in space). The dust particles acquire high charges and interact with each other. A plasma that contains larger particles is called grain plasma. Under laboratory conditions, dusty plasmas are also called
       <i>
        complex plasmas
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Impermeable_plasma">
        Impermeable plasma
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Impermeable plasma">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Impermeable plasma is a type of thermal plasma which acts like an impermeable solid with respect to gas or cold plasma and can be physically pushed. Interaction of cold gas and thermal plasma was briefly studied by a group led by
       <a href="/wiki/Hannes_Alfv%C3%A9n" title="Hannes Alfvén">
        Hannes Alfvén
       </a>
       in 1960s and 1970s for its possible applications in insulation of
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
        fusion
       </a>
       plasma from the reactor walls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, later it was found that the external
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_fields" title="Magnetic fields">
        magnetic fields
       </a>
       in this configuration could induce
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kink_instabilities&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kink instabilities (page does not exist)">
        kink instabilities
       </a>
       in the plasma and subsequently lead to an unexpectedly high heat loss to the walls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013, a group of materials scientists reported that they have successfully generated stable impermeable plasma with no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_confinement" title="Magnetic confinement">
        magnetic confinement
       </a>
       using only an ultrahigh-pressure blanket of cold gas. While spectroscopic data on the characteristics of plasma were claimed to be difficult to obtain due to the high pressure, the passive effect of plasma on
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">
        synthesis
       </a>
       of different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanostructures" title="Nanostructures">
        nanostructures
       </a>
       clearly suggested the effective confinement. They also showed that upon maintaining the impermeability for a few tens of seconds, screening of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">
        ions
       </a>
       at the plasma-gas interface could give rise to a strong secondary mode of heating (known as viscous heating) leading to different kinetics of reactions and formation of complex
       <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">
        nanomaterials
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_descriptions">
        Mathematical descriptions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mathematical descriptions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_rope.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="262" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/220px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/330px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/440px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_rope.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The complex self-constricting magnetic field lines and current paths in a field-aligned
         <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">
          Birkeland current
         </a>
         that can develop in a plasma.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
          <a href="#cite_note-59">
           [59]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_modeling" title="Plasma modeling">
        Plasma modeling
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       To completely describe the state of a plasma, all of the particle locations and velocities and describe the electromagnetic field in the plasma region would need to be written down. However, it is generally not practical or necessary to keep track of all the particles in a plasma. Therefore, plasma physicists commonly use less detailed descriptions, of which there are two main types:
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fluid_model">
        Fluid model
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fluid model">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Fluid models describe plasmas in terms of smoothed quantities, like density and averaged velocity around each position (see
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_parameters" title="Plasma parameters">
        Plasma parameters
       </a>
       ). One simple fluid model,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">
        magnetohydrodynamics
       </a>
       , treats the plasma as a single fluid governed by a combination of
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations" title="Navier–Stokes equations">
        Navier–Stokes equations
       </a>
       . A more general description is the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Two-fluid_plasma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Two-fluid plasma (page does not exist)">
        two-fluid plasma
       </a>
       picture, where the ions and electrons are described separately. Fluid models are often accurate when collisionality is sufficiently high to keep the plasma velocity distribution close to a
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
        Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution
       </a>
       . Because fluid models usually describe the plasma in terms of a single flow at a certain temperature at each spatial location, they can neither capture velocity space structures like beams or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_layer_(plasma)" title="Double layer (plasma)">
        double layers
       </a>
       , nor resolve wave-particle effects.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kinetic_model">
        Kinetic model
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Kinetic model">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Kinetic models describe the particle velocity distribution function at each point in the plasma and therefore do not need to assume a
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
        Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution
       </a>
       . A kinetic description is often necessary for collisionless plasmas. There are two common approaches to kinetic description of a plasma. One is based on representing the smoothed distribution function on a grid in velocity and position. The other, known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Particle-in-cell" title="Particle-in-cell">
        particle-in-cell
       </a>
       (PIC) technique, includes kinetic information by following the trajectories of a large number of individual particles. Kinetic models are generally more computationally intensive than fluid models. The
       <a href="/wiki/Vlasov_equation" title="Vlasov equation">
        Vlasov equation
       </a>
       may be used to describe the dynamics of a system of charged particles interacting with an electromagnetic field. In magnetized plasmas, a
       <a href="/wiki/Gyrokinetics" title="Gyrokinetics">
        gyrokinetic
       </a>
       approach can substantially reduce the computational expense of a fully kinetic simulation.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_plasmas">
        Artificial plasmas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Artificial plasmas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Most artificial plasmas are generated by the application of electric and/or magnetic fields through a gas. Plasma generated in a laboratory setting and for industrial use can be generally categorized by:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The type of power source used to generate the plasma—DC, RF and microwave
       </li>
       <li>
        The pressure they operate at—vacuum pressure (&lt; 10 mTorr or 1 Pa), moderate pressure (≈1 Torr or 100 Pa), atmospheric pressure (760 Torr or 100 kPa)
       </li>
       <li>
        The degree of ionisation within the plasma—fully, partially, or weakly ionised
       </li>
       <li>
        The temperature relationships within the plasma—thermal plasma (
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle T_{e}=T_{i}=T_{gas}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 i
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 g
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 a
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 s
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle T_{e}=T_{i}=T_{gas}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="T_e = T_i = T_{gas}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/559abad0312505020e0fadd7bdc671e2d12896fc" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:14.73ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
        ), non-thermal or "cold" plasma (
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle T_{e}\gg T_{i}=T_{gas}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 e
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               ≫
               <!-- ≫ -->
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 i
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 g
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 a
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 s
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle T_{e}\gg T_{i}=T_{gas}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="T_e \gg T_i = T_{gas}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/485dd6e8a33089d7998697ef5884d77d7052f760" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:15.245ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        The electrode configuration used to generate the plasma
       </li>
       <li>
        The magnetization of the particles within the plasma—magnetized (both ion and electrons are trapped in
        <a href="/wiki/Gyroradius" title="Gyroradius">
         Larmor orbits
        </a>
        by the magnetic field), partially magnetized (the electrons but not the ions are trapped by the magnetic field), non-magnetized (the magnetic field is too weak to trap the particles in orbits but may generate
        <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">
         Lorentz forces
        </a>
        )
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Generation_of_artificial_plasma">
        Generation of artificial plasma
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Generation of artificial plasma">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png">
         <img alt="Simple representation of a discharge tube - plasma.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="400" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png/220px-Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png/330px-Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simple_representation_of_a_discharge_tube_-_plasma.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg">
         <img alt="Artificial plasma produced in air by a Jacob's Ladder" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1762" data-file-width="2642" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg/220px-Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg/330px-Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg/440px-Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_jacobs_ladder.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artificial plasma produced in air by a
         <a href="/wiki/Spark_gap#Visual_entertainment" title="Spark gap">
          Jacob's Ladder
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Just like the many uses of plasma, there are several means for its generation, however, one principle is common to all of them: there must be energy input to produce and sustain it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hippler_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hippler-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For this case, plasma is generated when an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
        electric current
       </a>
       is applied across a
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_gas" title="Dielectric gas">
        dielectric gas
       </a>
       or fluid (an electrically
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">
        non-conducting
       </a>
       material) as can be seen in the adjacent image, which shows a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discharge_tube" title="Discharge tube">
        discharge tube
       </a>
       as a simple example (
       <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">
        DC
       </a>
       used for simplicity).
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potential_difference" title="Potential difference">
        potential difference
       </a>
       and subsequent
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric field
       </a>
       pull the bound electrons (negative) toward the
       <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">
        anode
       </a>
       (positive electrode) while the
       <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">
        cathode
       </a>
       (negative electrode) pulls the nucleus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chen-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the
       <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">
        voltage
       </a>
       increases, the current stresses the material (by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_polarization" title="Electric polarization">
        electric polarization
       </a>
       ) beyond its
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_strength" title="Dielectric strength">
        dielectric limit
       </a>
       (termed strength) into a stage of
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_breakdown" title="Electrical breakdown">
        electrical breakdown
       </a>
       , marked by an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_spark" title="Electric spark">
        electric spark
       </a>
       , where the material transforms from being an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insulator_(electrical)" title="Insulator (electrical)">
        insulator
       </a>
       into a
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">
        conductor
       </a>
       (as it becomes increasingly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionized" title="Ionized">
        ionised
       </a>
       ). The underlying process is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Townsend_avalanche" title="Townsend avalanche">
        Townsend avalanche
       </a>
       , where collisions between electrons and neutral gas atoms create more ions and electrons (as can be seen in the figure on the right). The first impact of an electron on an atom results in one ion and two electrons. Therefore, the number of charged particles increases rapidly (in the millions) only "after about 20 successive sets of collisions",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leal-Quiros_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Leal-Quiros-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mainly due to a small mean free path (average distance travelled between collisions).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_arc">
        Electric arc
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Electric arc">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="617" data-file-width="512" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg/220px-Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg/330px-Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg/440px-Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cascade-process-of-ionization.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Cascade process of ionisation. Electrons are ‘e−’, neutral atoms ‘o’, and cations ‘+’.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Townsend_Discharge.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="311" data-file-width="476" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Townsend_Discharge.svg/300px-Townsend_Discharge.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Townsend_Discharge.svg/450px-Townsend_Discharge.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Townsend_Discharge.svg/600px-Townsend_Discharge.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Townsend_Discharge.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Avalanche effect between two electrodes. The original ionisation event liberates one electron, and each subsequent collision liberates a further electron, so two electrons emerge from each collision: the ionising electron and the liberated electron.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       With ample current density and ionisation, this forms a luminous
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">
        electric arc
       </a>
       (a continuous electric discharge similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">
        lightning
       </a>
       ) between the electrodes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [Note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">
        Electrical resistance
       </a>
       along the continuous electric arc creates
       <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">
        heat
       </a>
       , which dissociates more gas molecules and ionises the resulting atoms (where degree of ionisation is determined by temperature), and as per the sequence:
       <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
        solid
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">
        liquid
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">
        gas
       </a>
       -plasma, the gas is gradually turned into a thermal plasma.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [Note 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A thermal plasma is in
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">
        thermal equilibrium
       </a>
       , which is to say that the temperature is relatively homogeneous throughout the heavy particles (i.e. atoms, molecules and ions) and electrons. This is so because when thermal plasmas are generated,
       <a href="/wiki/Electrical_energy" title="Electrical energy">
        electrical energy
       </a>
       is given to electrons, which, due to their great mobility and large numbers, are able to disperse it rapidly and by
       <a href="/wiki/Elastic_collision" title="Elastic collision">
        elastic collision
       </a>
       (without energy loss) to the heavy particles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gomez-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [Note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Examples_of_industrial.2Fcommercial_plasma">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_industrial/commercial_plasma">
        Examples of industrial/commercial plasma
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Examples of industrial/commercial plasma">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Because of their sizable temperature and density ranges, plasmas find applications in many fields of research, technology and industry. For example, in: industrial and extractive
       <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">
        metallurgy
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez_64-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gomez-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szałatkiewicz_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Szałatkiewicz-67">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       surface treatments such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_spraying" title="Plasma spraying">
        plasma spraying
       </a>
       (coating),
       <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">
        etching
       </a>
       in microelectronics,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NRC-68">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       metal cutting
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nemchinsky_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nemchinsky-69">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">
        welding
       </a>
       ; as well as in everyday
       <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_emissions_control" title="Vehicle emissions control">
        vehicle exhaust cleanup
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
        fluorescent
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Electroluminescence" title="Electroluminescence">
        luminescent
       </a>
       lamps,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hippler_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hippler-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , fuel ignition, while even playing a part in
       <a href="/wiki/Scramjet" title="Scramjet">
        supersonic combustion engines
       </a>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">
        aerospace engineering
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peretich_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Peretich-70">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Low-pressure_discharges">
        Low-pressure discharges
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Low-pressure discharges">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Glow_discharge" title="Glow discharge">
          Glow discharge
         </a>
         plasmas
        </i>
        : non-thermal plasmas generated by the application of DC or low frequency RF (&lt;100 kHz) electric field to the gap between two metal electrodes. Probably the most common plasma; this is the type of plasma generated within
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" title="Fluorescent light">
         fluorescent light
        </a>
        tubes.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
         <a href="#cite_note-71">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Capacitively_coupled_plasma" title="Capacitively coupled plasma">
          Capacitively coupled plasma
         </a>
         (CCP)
        </i>
        : similar to glow discharge plasmas, but generated with high frequency RF electric fields, typically
        <a href="/wiki/ISM_band" title="ISM band">
         13.56 MHz
        </a>
        . These differ from glow discharges in that the sheaths are much less intense. These are widely used in the microfabrication and integrated circuit manufacturing industries for plasma etching and plasma enhanced chemical vapor deposition.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
         <a href="#cite_note-72">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Cascaded_Arc_Plasma_Source" title="Cascaded Arc Plasma Source">
          Cascaded Arc Plasma Source
         </a>
        </i>
        : a device to produce low temperature (≈1eV) high density plasmas (HDP).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Inductively_coupled_plasma" title="Inductively coupled plasma">
          Inductively coupled plasma
         </a>
         (ICP)
        </i>
        : similar to a CCP and with similar applications but the electrode consists of a coil wrapped around the chamber where plasma is formed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
         <a href="#cite_note-73">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wave_heated_plasma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wave heated plasma (page does not exist)">
          Wave heated plasma
         </a>
        </i>
        : similar to CCP and ICP in that it is typically RF (or microwave). Examples include
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helicon_discharge" title="Helicon discharge">
         helicon discharge
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Electron_cyclotron_resonance" title="Electron cyclotron resonance">
         electron cyclotron resonance
        </a>
        (ECR).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
         <a href="#cite_note-74">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_pressure">
        Atmospheric pressure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Atmospheric pressure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arc_discharge" title="Arc discharge">
          Arc discharge
         </a>
         :
        </i>
        this is a high power thermal discharge of very high temperature (≈10,000 K). It can be generated using various power supplies. It is commonly used in
        <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">
         metallurgical
        </a>
        processes. For example, it is used to smelt minerals containing Al
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        O
        <sub>
         3
        </sub>
        to produce
        <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">
         aluminium
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Corona_discharge" title="Corona discharge">
          Corona discharge
         </a>
         :
        </i>
        this is a non-thermal discharge generated by the application of high voltage to sharp electrode tips. It is commonly used in
        <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">
         ozone
        </a>
        generators and particle precipitators.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_barrier_discharge" title="Dielectric barrier discharge">
          Dielectric barrier discharge
         </a>
         (DBD):
        </i>
        this is a non-thermal discharge generated by the application of high voltages across small gaps wherein a non-conducting coating prevents the transition of the plasma discharge into an arc. It is often mislabeled 'Corona' discharge in industry and has similar application to corona discharges. It is also widely used in the web treatment of fabrics.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
         <a href="#cite_note-75">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The application of the discharge to synthetic fabrics and plastics functionalizes the surface and allows for paints, glues and similar materials to adhere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
         <a href="#cite_note-76">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The dielectric barrier discharge was used in the mid-1990s to show that low temperature atmospheric pressure plasma is effective in inactivating bacterial cells.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This work and later experiments using mammalian cells led to the establishment of a new field of research known as
        <a href="/wiki/Plasma_medicine" title="Plasma medicine">
         plasma medicine
        </a>
        . The dielectric barrier discharge configuration was also used in the design of low temperature plasma jets. These plasma jets are produced by fast propagating guided ionisation waves known as plasma bullets.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
         <a href="#cite_note-78">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Capacitive_discharge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Capacitive discharge (page does not exist)">
          Capacitive discharge
         </a>
         :
        </i>
        this is a
        <a href="/wiki/Nonthermal_plasma" title="Nonthermal plasma">
         nonthermal plasma
        </a>
        generated by the application of RF power (e.g.,
        <a href="/wiki/ISM_band" title="ISM band">
         13.56 MHz
        </a>
        ) to one powered electrode, with a grounded electrode held at a small separation distance on the order of 1 cm. Such discharges are commonly stabilized using a noble gas such as helium or argon.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        "
        <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric_direct_discharge_plasma" title="Piezoelectric direct discharge plasma">
         Piezoelectric direct discharge plasma
        </a>
        :" is a
        <a href="/wiki/Nonthermal_plasma" title="Nonthermal plasma">
         nonthermal plasma
        </a>
        generated at the high-side of a piezoelectric transformer (PT). This generation variant is particularly suited for high efficient and compact devices where a separate high voltage power supply is not desired.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Research">
        Research
        <span id="Fields_of_active_research">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Research">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Plasmas are the object of study of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_field" title="Academic field">
        academic field
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        plasma science
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        plasma physics
       </i>
       , including sub-disciplines such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_plasma_physics" title="Space plasma physics">
        space plasma physics
       </a>
       . It currently involves the following fields of active research and features across many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_journals" title="Academic journals">
        journals
       </a>
       , whose interest includes:
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Research_examples">
        Research examples
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Research examples">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:30px auto;">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HallThruster_2.jpg">
            <img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="533" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/HallThruster_2.jpg/120px-HallThruster_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/HallThruster_2.jpg/180px-HallThruster_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/HallThruster_2.jpg/240px-HallThruster_2.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hall_effect_thruster" title="Hall effect thruster">
            Hall effect thruster
           </a>
           . The electric field in a plasma
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_layer_(plasma)" title="Double layer (plasma)">
            double layer
           </a>
           is so effective at accelerating ions that electric fields are used in
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_drive" title="Ion drive">
            ion drives
           </a>
           .
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
        <div style="width: 155px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:15px auto;">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Plasma_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="mw-references-wrap">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-63">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-63">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The material undergoes various ‘regimes’ or stages (e.g. saturation, breakdown, glow, transition and thermal arc) as the voltage is increased under the voltage-current relationship. The voltage rises to its maximum value in the saturation stage, and thereafter it undergoes fluctuations of the various stages; while the current progressively increases throughout.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leal-Quiros_62-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Leal-Quiros-62">
            [62]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-65">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-65">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Across literature, there appears to be no strict definition on where the boundary is between a gas and plasma. Nevertheless, it is enough to say that at 2,000°C the gas molecules become atomized, and ionised at 3,000 °C and "in this state, [the] gas has a liquid like viscosity at atmospheric pressure and the free electric charges confer relatively high electrical conductivities that can approach those of metals."
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez_64-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Gomez-64">
            [63]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-66">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-66">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Note that non-thermal, or non-equilibrium plasmas are not as ionised and have lower energy densities, and thus the temperature is not dispersed evenly among the particles, where some heavy ones remain ‘cold’.
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>
      </div>
